# Pacifier Use

## THE QUICK ANSWER

Pacifiers are safe—and may even reduce SIDS risk—once breastfeeding is established (usually 3-4 weeks). The "nipple confusion" concern is largely overstated. If you want to use one, wait until nursing is going well, then introduce without guilt.

## WHAT IT IS

Pacifiers satisfy a baby's natural urge to suck for comfort rather than nutrition. The debate centers on timing: Will early pacifier use interfere with breastfeeding? What do hospitals allow?

## THE EVIDENCE

**The nipple confusion debate:**
- Theory: Pacifiers teach different sucking mechanics that make breastfeeding harder
- Reality: A Cochrane review found pacifier use in healthy breastfeeding infants did not significantly affect breastfeeding duration when introduced after nursing was established
- The association between pacifiers and shorter breastfeeding may be correlation, not causation—mothers struggling with nursing may turn to pacifiers more often

**SIDS risk reduction:**
- Multiple studies show pacifier use during sleep reduces SIDS risk by 50-90%
- The AAP recommends offering a pacifier at naptime and bedtime once breastfeeding is established
- This is one of the strongest arguments for pacifiers

**Other considerations:**
- Extended use (beyond age 2-3) is associated with dental problems
- May slightly increase ear infection risk
- Provides genuine comfort and helps babies self-soothe

**Hospital policies:**
- Baby-Friendly hospitals often restrict pacifiers
- These policies are based on older research and are increasingly questioned
- You can request one regardless of hospital certification

## OUR TAKE

Wait until breastfeeding is going well (3-4 weeks), then feel free to offer a pacifier. The SIDS protection alone makes them worth considering for sleep.

**Not breastfeeding?** No reason to wait. Use one from day one if you want.

**At the hospital:** You can request a pacifier even at Baby-Friendly facilities. It's your choice.

**If baby refuses:** Many do. Don't force it.

Plan to wean by age 2-3 to avoid dental issues, but that's future you's problem.

Pacifiers are a tool, not a failure.


---


# Rooming In

## THE QUICK ANSWER

Rooming in (keeping baby in your room 24/7) has real benefits for bonding and breastfeeding. But it can be exhausting when you're recovering from birth. If a nursery is available and you need rest, using it doesn't make you a bad parent.

## WHAT IT IS

Rooming in means your newborn stays with you around the clock rather than spending time in the hospital nursery. Most hospitals now default to this. Many Baby-Friendly hospitals have eliminated nurseries entirely.

## THE EVIDENCE

**Benefits of rooming in:**
- Better breastfeeding: Feeding on demand is easier when baby is right there
- Bonding: Constant proximity triggers bonding hormones
- Learning baby's cues: You observe patterns before going home
- Some research suggests it may reduce hospital errors

**The exhaustion reality:**
- You just gave birth—your body needs recovery
- Newborns feed every 2-3 hours, leaving little sustained sleep
- Hospital rooms have constant interruptions: vitals, meals, staff
- Sleep deprivation is linked to postpartum mood disorders
- Partners often can't stay comfortably, leaving you alone overnight

**What research shows:** While rooming in supports breastfeeding, studies don't account well for maternal exhaustion and mental health. A few hours of nursery care so you can sleep has not been shown to harm outcomes.

## OUR TAKE

Rooming in is wonderful in theory—but not a moral imperative. Your baby needs you healthy, not depleted.

**If you have nursery access:** Use it when you need it. A 3-4 hour sleep stretch is transformative. Ask them to bring baby for feedings.

**If there's no nursery:** Ask if nurses can watch baby at the station. Have your partner take shifts.

**What actually matters:** Skin-to-skin when awake. Responsive feeding. Rest when you can. These aren't mutually exclusive with occasional nursery time.

There is no shame in needing rest. A rested parent is a safer, more present parent.


---


# Circumcision: Making the Decision

## One-Page Overview

Circumcision is the surgical removal of the foreskin, the tissue covering the head of the penis. In the United States, about 55-60% of newborn boys are circumcised, though this rate has been declining. Outside the U.S., routine infant circumcision is uncommon in most developed countries.

The medical evidence shows some health benefits exist, but they are modest. Circumcision reduces urinary tract infections (though you would need to circumcise about 100 boys to prevent one UTI). Claims about HIV prevention come from studies in Africa and may not apply to developed countries. Penile cancer is so rare (less than 1 in 100,000 men) that prevention is not a practical benefit.

Risks include bleeding, infection, and pain. Serious complications are rare but documented. The procedure is painful and pain management varies widely between hospitals.

**Bottom line:** No major medical organization recommends routine circumcision for all newborns. No major medical organization says it should never be done. The American Academy of Pediatrics (AAP) concluded that benefits outweigh risks, but not enough to recommend the procedure universally. The decision is left to parents based on their values, culture, religion, and individual circumstances.

---

## What Is Circumcision?

### The Procedure

Circumcision is surgery to remove the foreskin, the retractable fold of skin that covers the head (glans) of the penis. It is typically performed in the hospital within the first few days of life and takes about 5-10 minutes.

Several methods are used:

- **Gomco clamp:** The foreskin is drawn through a bell-shaped device. A clamp cuts off blood flow, and the foreskin is trimmed away. This takes 3-5 minutes.
- **Mogen clamp:** A hinged clamp holds the foreskin while cutting. This is faster, usually 1-2 minutes.
- **Plastibell device:** A plastic ring is placed under the foreskin and a thread is tied around it, cutting off blood supply. The ring falls off in 5-8 days as the tissue separates.

### What Tissue Is Removed

The foreskin is not a leftover piece of tissue. It serves several functions:

- **Protection:** The foreskin covers and protects the sensitive head of the penis during childhood and adulthood.
- **Sensation:** The foreskin contains fine-touch receptors and specialized nerve endings. Studies show it is among the most sensitive parts of the external penis.
- **Function during sex:** The foreskin moves during sexual activity, reducing friction.
- **Development:** In newborns, the foreskin is naturally fused to the head of the penis. This protects the developing tissue. The foreskin separates naturally over time, typically between ages 3-5.

---

## Medical Organization Positions

### AAP 2012 Position

The American Academy of Pediatrics issued its most recent policy statement on circumcision in 2012. This statement is often misunderstood.

**What the AAP says:**
- The health benefits of newborn male circumcision outweigh the risks
- The benefits justify insurance coverage for the procedure
- The AAP does *not* recommend routine circumcision for all newborns

**What this actually means:** The AAP found that some health benefits exist, but benefits minus risks equals a small number. Small enough that the AAP decided not to recommend the procedure universally. Instead, they left the choice to parents.

Dr. Andrew Freedman, who served on the task force that wrote the policy, explained: "There's no right or wrong answer. There are some minimal benefits and some minimal risks. There's not enough benefit to say you must do it, and there's not enough risk to say you can never do it."

Important: This policy expired in 2017 and has not been updated. The 2012 statement remains the AAP's most recent guidance.

### International Perspectives

Outside the United States, medical organizations take a different view:

- **Royal Dutch Medical Association (Netherlands):** Concluded there is "no convincing evidence that circumcision is useful or necessary in terms of prevention or hygiene." The organization opposes non-therapeutic circumcision in children.
- **Danish Medical Association:** Stated that "the current practice of circumcising boys without a medical indication should cease."
- **British Medical Association:** Noted that "parental preference alone does not constitute sufficient grounds" for circumcision and that evidence of health benefits is insufficient.
- **German pediatric organizations:** Multiple German medical associations oppose routine non-medical infant circumcision.

The key difference: American medicine focuses on potential disease prevention. European medicine focuses on whether diseases are actually common enough to justify the procedure. In countries with HPV vaccination, treatable UTIs, and very rare penile cancer, routine circumcision is harder to justify medically.

**Key fact:** No major medical organization in the world recommends routine circumcision for all male infants. No major medical organization recommends banning it. The procedure occupies a middle ground: it is an option, not a requirement.

---

## The Claimed Benefits

### UTI Reduction: The Real Numbers

Urinary tract infection prevention is the most well-supported benefit.

**What the research shows:**
- Circumcision reduces UTI risk. Uncircumcised boys have about 10 times higher risk of UTI in infancy compared to circumcised boys.
- This protective effect decreases with age.

**But here are the real numbers:**
- To prevent one UTI in a healthy boy, you need to circumcise approximately 100-111 boys.
- To prevent one UTI serious enough to require hospitalization, about 195 boys need to be circumcised.
- Overall UTI risk in healthy uncircumcised boys is about 1-2% by age 5.
- UTIs in boys are almost always easily treated with antibiotics.

**When this matters more:** Boys with certain urological conditions benefit more from circumcision. For boys with recurrent UTIs, the number needed to treat drops to about 11. For boys with vesicoureteric reflux (urine backing up into kidneys), only about 4 circumcisions prevent one UTI.

### HIV/STI Prevention: The African Studies and Their Limitations

Three major studies in South Africa, Kenya, and Uganda found that circumcision reduced HIV infection rates in heterosexual men by about 60%. This led the World Health Organization to recommend voluntary medical male circumcision in high-prevalence African countries.

**Critical limitations:**
1. These studies were done in settings with extremely high HIV rates where heterosexual transmission is the main route of infection.
2. The procedure was performed on adolescents and adults who consented, not newborns.
3. There is no evidence that circumcision reduces transmission from men to women.
4. There is no evidence of protection for male-to-male transmission.
5. Population-level data from several African countries showed circumcised men were actually more likely to be HIV-positive than intact men.
6. Whether these findings apply to newborn circumcision in low-prevalence developed countries has not been established.

**For other STIs:** The evidence is weaker than for HIV. HPV vaccines (Gardasil, Cervarix) prevent HPV-related cancers far more effectively than circumcision.

### Penile Cancer: Extremely Rare

Penile cancer is sometimes cited as a reason for circumcision.

**How rare is it?**
- Less than 1 case per 100,000 men per year in developed countries
- Less than 1% of all male cancers
- Average age at diagnosis: 60 years old

For comparison, prostate cancer affects 288,000 American men per year. Penile cancer affects fewer than 2,000.

Circumcision may provide some protection, but this appears to work mainly by preventing phimosis (inability to retract the foreskin), which is a risk factor for penile cancer. When studies looked only at men without phimosis, circumcision showed no protective effect.

HPV vaccination prevents HPV-related penile cancer far more effectively and is recommended for all teens.

### Critical Analysis

When examining these benefits together:
- UTI prevention is real but modest (100 circumcisions to prevent one infection)
- HIV prevention data comes from a very different context and may not apply
- Penile cancer is too rare to be a meaningful factor
- Better alternatives exist for most claimed benefits (antibiotics for UTIs, condoms and vaccines for STIs, vaccines for HPV-related cancers)

---

## The Risks and Considerations

### Complication Rates

Circumcision is surgery, and all surgery carries risks.

**Overall complication rate: 1.5-3% in most studies** (though rates from 0-16% have been reported depending on how complications are counted)

**Common complications:**
- **Bleeding:** The most frequent complication. Usually minor and stops with gentle pressure. Occurs in about 0.5-2% of circumcisions.
- **Infection:** Usually minor and treatable with antibiotics. Occurs in about 0.5-1% of circumcisions.
- **Meatal stenosis:** Narrowing of the urethral opening, which may cause difficulty urinating. Occurs in about 0.3-1% of circumcisions.

**Serious complications (rare but documented):**
- Severe infection
- Excessive bleeding requiring intervention
- Damage to the penis
- Death (extremely rare, estimated at fewer than 1 per 500,000)

Complications are less likely when the provider is experienced, the procedure is done in a medical setting, and it is performed in the newborn period.

### What the Foreskin Does

The foreskin is not useless tissue:
- It protects the sensitive head of the penis
- It contains specialized nerve endings for fine-touch sensation
- It moves during sexual activity, reducing friction
- In newborns, it is fused to the head of the penis to protect developing tissue

### Sensitivity Research

Research on whether circumcision affects sexual sensation is mixed and contested.

**Studies finding reduced sensitivity:** A 2013 study found that the foreskin has significant sensory function and that circumcised men reported decreased sexual pleasure and greater difficulty achieving orgasm.

**Studies finding no effect:** A 2016 systematic review of 40,473 men found no overall adverse effect on penile sensitivity, sexual arousal, erectile function, or orgasm.

**Why the contradiction?** Study methods vary dramatically. How people feel about their circumcision status may influence their reported satisfaction more than the circumcision itself.

The honest answer: Circumcision removes sensitive tissue, but whether this affects sexual satisfaction is unclear and may vary by individual.

### Pain Management

Circumcision causes significant pain. Studies show the procedure increases heart rate, stress hormones, and blood pressure. Some babies cry intensely; others go into a shutdown state.

**Pain management varies widely:**
- Some hospitals routinely offer local anesthetic
- Others offer it only if parents request it
- Some do not offer any pain relief

When pain management is used, it typically includes:
- Dorsal penile nerve block (injection near the base of the penis)
- Ring block (injection around the penis)
- Topical anesthetic cream (less effective than injection)
- Sucrose or other comfort measures

Even with anesthesia, pain is reduced but not eliminated.

### The Trauma Question: Early Life Pain and Development

This is a difficult topic, but one that deserves honest discussion.

**What happens physiologically during circumcision:**
- Cortisol (stress hormone) levels elevate 3-4x and remain elevated for hours to days afterward
- Heart rate and blood pressure increase significantly
- Researchers have documented decreased wakefulness, increased vagal tone, and reduced environmental interaction following the procedure

**Research on altered pain processing:**
A study comparing circumcised and uncircumcised infants found that circumcised boys showed significantly stronger pain responses to routine vaccinations months later. The researchers suggested this represents an "infant analogue" of altered stress response—the early pain experience changed how their nervous systems process subsequent pain.

**The first hours and days matter:**
Research published in *Frontiers in Pediatrics* shows that the developing brain is particularly sensitive to procedural pain during a "critical window" in early life. The brain regions most affected are those connected to the limbic system—the areas governing emotion, memory, and stress response.

While we cannot ask a newborn how they experience circumcision, we can measure their physiological responses. Those responses indicate significant stress. Whether this constitutes "trauma" in the psychological sense, and whether it has lasting effects, remains debated.

**Studies with conflicting findings:**

*Studies suggesting long-term effects:*
- A study of 408 circumcised and 211 non-circumcised men found that early-circumcised men reported lower attachment security and lower emotional stability
- Research by Ramos and Boyle found that 51% of boys circumcised in a hospital setting met diagnostic criteria for PTSD, though this study has methodological limitations

*Studies finding no long-term effects:*
- Other studies have found no statistically significant differences in psychological measures between circumcised and intact men
- Many circumcised men report no negative psychological effects

**The honest assessment:**
The research is mixed and methodologically challenging. What we can say with confidence:
1. Circumcision is painful and stressful for infants
2. Early life pain can affect developing brain regions and pain processing
3. Whether circumcision specifically causes lasting psychological harm is not definitively established
4. The experience varies based on pain management, individual infant, and circumstances

**What this means for your decision:**
If you choose circumcision, ensure adequate pain management is provided. The procedure should not be performed without anesthesia. Consider timing (day 8 may be optimal). Understand that your baby will experience pain and stress, even with pain relief.

If you're uncertain whether the medical benefits justify subjecting your newborn to a painful surgical procedure in his first days of life, that uncertainty is reasonable. Many parents conclude the answer is no.

---

## Cultural and Religious Considerations

### Jewish Tradition (Day 8, Vitamin K)

In Jewish tradition, circumcision (brit milah, or "bris") is performed on the 8th day of life by a trained circumciser called a mohel.

**Why day 8 matters medically:** By day 8, an infant's blood clotting ability has naturally peaked. Vitamin K levels rise after birth and reach their highest point around day 8. Clotting factors reach and exceed adult levels. This reduces bleeding risk.

This is remarkable: the ancient tradition essentially identified the optimal timing for blood clotting without modern medical knowledge.

Jewish law allows postponement if the baby is ill, and specifies that if two brothers died from circumcision complications, the third brother should not be circumcised. This guidance is consistent with concern about hemophilia, a genetic bleeding disorder.

Traditional brit milah involves minimal pain management, though many modern mohelim now use anesthesia.

### Muslim Tradition

In Islam, circumcision (khitan) is considered important but is not mandated in the Quran. It appears in hadith (reports of the Prophet Muhammad's teachings) and is viewed as highly recommended.

Timing varies widely: days after birth, around age 6-7, or later childhood. There is no single standard across Muslim communities.

### American Cultural Norms

In the United States, circumcision became a cultural norm in the 20th century for non-religious reasons:
- In 1900, fewer than 5% of American boys were circumcised
- By 1970, about 80-90% were circumcised
- This shift was driven by medical claims (many later disproven) about hygiene and disease prevention

### Declining Rates

Circumcision rates are falling:
- Current U.S. rate: approximately 55-60% (down from 80-90% in the 1970s)
- Western states: 30-40%
- Midwest and South: 60-70%
- Rates are lower among Hispanic and Asian American families

For families without religious or cultural reasons, the trend is toward leaving infants intact.

---

## Ethical Considerations

### Bodily Autonomy

The central ethical tension is between parental rights and the child's right to bodily integrity.

**The parental authority view:** Parents have the right to make medical decisions for their children. This authority is grounded in the assumption that parents act in their child's best interests.

**The bodily autonomy view:** Many ethicists argue that:
- Circumcision is an irreversible surgical alteration of a healthy child who cannot consent
- The procedure is not medically necessary to save the child's life or treat a disease
- The decision could be deferred until the child is old enough to decide for himself

Several medical organizations have taken positions emphasizing the child's right to bodily integrity:
- The Swedish Medical Association recommended setting 12 as the minimum age and requiring the child's consent
- The Danish Medical Association emphasized the child's right to self-determination
- A German court ruled in 2012 that circumcision of a non-consenting boy constitutes assault, even for religious reasons (this sparked international debate)

### Irreversibility

Unlike many medical decisions, circumcision is permanent:
- The foreskin cannot be regrown
- Any change to sensation is permanent
- The altered anatomy is permanent

Some ethicists argue that medical procedures on children should meet this standard: "Would the child likely choose this for himself as an adult?" For most medical decisions (vaccines, treating infections, setting broken bones), the answer is clearly yes. For circumcision without religious reasons, the answer is less clear.

---

## Making Your Decision

### If You Choose Circumcision

**Timing:**
- Day 8 is considered optimal (aligns with peak blood clotting and Jewish tradition)
- Days 1-3 are acceptable but clotting has not yet peaked
- The newborn period (first 1-2 weeks) is considered safest
- Waiting past the newborn period is possible but more complicated

**Pain management - ask for it explicitly:**
- Request a local anesthetic
- Dorsal penile nerve block or ring block is most effective
- If your provider does not offer anesthesia, ask why and consider another provider
- Even with anesthesia, comfort measures (holding, sugar water) help

**Provider selection:**
- Ask about your provider's experience and complication rate
- If using a religious circumciser, ask about training and pain management
- Verify the procedure will be performed in a sterile environment

**Aftercare:**
- Follow your provider's specific instructions
- Change diapers frequently to keep the area clean and dry
- Gently clean with warm water
- Apply any prescribed ointment
- Contact a doctor for excessive bleeding, signs of infection (redness, swelling, pus, fever), or inability to urinate

### If You Decline: Care of the Intact Penis

Caring for an uncircumcised penis is actually simpler than caring for a circumcised one.

**Newborn care:**
- Wash with warm water only (no soap, no cotton swabs)
- The foreskin is fused to the head and will not retract. This is normal.
- **Never force the foreskin back**
- Clean only what is visible

**What is normal:**
- The foreskin will not retract in infancy
- A white or yellowish substance (smegma) under the foreskin is normal and protective
- Small pearl-like bumps under the foreskin are normal

**As your child grows (ages 1-5):**
- The foreskin gradually separates naturally
- This happens at different ages (can take years)
- Never force retraction
- Care stays the same: warm water only

**When the foreskin is fully retractable (usually age 3-5 or later):**
- Teach your son to gently pull back the foreskin and rinse with warm water during baths
- This becomes a normal part of hygiene, similar to how girls are taught to wipe

**The rule is simple:** "If intact, do not retract. Only clean what is seen."

**About phimosis:** Many parents worry about this. True phimosis (inability to retract that causes symptoms) occurs in only 1-2% of boys. If it does develop, first-line treatment is topical corticosteroid cream, which works in 75-90% of cases. Circumcision is not needed as prevention.

---

## Our Take

Circumcision is a personal decision that should be made thoughtfully, not automatically.

The medical evidence does not strongly support routine circumcision. The benefits are real but modest. The risks are usually minor but real. No major medical organization recommends the procedure for all newborns.

For families with religious or cultural traditions around circumcision, those traditions are valid reasons to proceed. For families without such traditions, the medical case alone is not compelling enough to recommend the procedure.

If you choose circumcision, ensure your baby receives adequate pain management and choose an experienced provider. If you choose not to circumcise, know that caring for an intact penis is straightforward.

Either choice is reasonable. What matters is that you understand the evidence, consider your values, and make an informed decision for your family.

---

# PART 8: GOING HOME

# When to Leave the Hospital

## THE QUICK ANSWER

Vaginal births typically stay 24-48 hours; C-sections stay 2-4 days. If you and baby are healthy, you can often leave earlier than the default. You can also stay longer if you need more recovery time or support.

## WHAT IT IS

Hospital discharge timing has shortened dramatically over decades. What used to be week-long stays are now 1-2 days for vaginal births. The question is: What's the right amount of time for YOU?

## THE EVIDENCE

**Typical stays:**
- Uncomplicated vaginal birth: 24-48 hours
- C-section: 2-4 days
- Complications: Varies based on situation

**What must happen before discharge:**
- Baby passes hearing screening
- Newborn screening (heel prick) completed
- Baby feeding adequately (milk coming in or colostrum sufficient)
- Baby urinating and stooling appropriately
- Pediatrician/provider examines baby
- Mother's bleeding is under control
- Mother can urinate on her own
- Mother has adequate pain management plan
- Car seat available

**Early discharge considerations:**
- Some healthy mothers and babies leave at 24 hours
- Requires follow-up appointment within 1-2 days
- Not recommended for first-time parents without strong support at home
- Must have pediatrician lined up for quick follow-up

**Reasons to stay longer:**
- Breastfeeding difficulties (lactation support available in hospital)
- Concerns about baby's jaundice
- Need for rest and recovery
- Limited support at home
- C-section recovery requiring more monitoring

**Insurance coverage:** The Newborns' and Mothers' Health Protection Act requires insurers to cover at least 48 hours for vaginal birth, 96 hours for C-section. You're entitled to this time if you want it.

## OUR TAKE

**Don't rush home** just because you can. The hospital has nurses, lactation consultants, and support. Use that time.

**Don't stay longer than you want** just because it's the default. If you're healthy, baby is healthy, and you have support at home, earlier discharge is fine.

**First-time parents:** Consider the full 48 hours. Breastfeeding help and newborn care guidance are valuable.

**Experienced parents:** You may be fine leaving earlier—you've done this before.

**After C-section:** Take the recovery time. Major surgery requires rest.

**Ask what you need:** "Can we have the lactation consultant visit again?" "Can we stay one more night?" Advocate for what serves your recovery.

You'll know when you're ready.


---


# First Days at Home

## THE QUICK ANSWER

The first days home are intense: exhaustion, hormones, healing, and learning a new human. This is survival mode. Focus on feeding the baby, resting when possible, and accepting all help offered. Everything else can wait.

## WHAT IT IS

You've left the hospital with a tiny human who depends entirely on you. There are no nurses down the hall anymore. This is the "fourth trimester"—the 12 weeks after birth when both you and baby are adjusting to this new world.

## THE EVIDENCE

**What to expect:**
- **Exhaustion:** Sleep deprivation is real and cumulative. Newborns eat every 2-3 hours around the clock.
- **Hormones:** Massive hormonal shifts happen in the first week. Mood swings, crying, and "baby blues" affect up to 80% of mothers.
- **Physical healing:** Vaginal soreness, lochia (bleeding), breast engorgement, C-section incision pain—your body is recovering from a major event.
- **Breastfeeding challenges:** Milk typically comes in days 2-5, which can be uncomfortable. Latching takes practice.

**Warning signs in MOM—call your provider:**
- Fever over 100.4°F
- Heavy bleeding (soaking a pad in an hour)
- Severe headache or vision changes
- Chest pain or difficulty breathing
- Painful, red, swollen area on leg (blood clot)
- Thoughts of harming yourself or baby
- Sadness/anxiety that doesn't improve after 2 weeks

**Warning signs in BABY—call pediatrician:**
- Fever over 100.4°F (rectal)
- Fewer than 6 wet diapers per day after day 4
- Yellow skin that worsens (jaundice)
- Difficulty breathing
- Won't wake to feed
- Inconsolable crying
- Umbilical cord area red, oozing, or foul-smelling

**The fourth trimester concept:** Your baby is still adjusting to life outside the womb. They need closeness, feeding on demand, and your presence. This isn't spoiling—it's biologically normal.

## OUR TAKE

**Accept help.** Every casserole, every offer to hold the baby while you shower, every load of laundry someone does for you. Say yes.

**Lower your standards.** The house will be messy. You might not shower daily. That's fine.

**Sleep when baby sleeps** is cliché because it's true. At least once a day, nap when baby naps.

**Limit visitors** who aren't helpful. If they're not bringing food or doing dishes, they can wait.

**Baby blues vs. PPD:** Baby blues (weepiness, mood swings) should improve within 2 weeks. If you're still struggling—or if you have thoughts of harm—that's postpartum depression and it's treatable. Tell someone.

This phase is hard. It's also temporary. You'll find your rhythm.


---
